<div id="review_1843668000" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1843668000" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41609617-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539156508p2/41609617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1843668000?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/41609617-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780008205218" href="/book/show/31008847-purple-hibiscus">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1843668000" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9817897091669536663">Whilst not quite in the same league as ‘Half of a Yellow Sun’ (‘Purple Hibiscus’ is neither as accomplished nor as ambitious in scope) – ‘Purple Hibiscus’ is nevertheless a very strong and affecting novel.<br/><br/>Set again in Nigeria and although told against a backdrop of civil unrest and corruption, this is very much focussed on the family and on the characters immediate domestic situation. Told by, and seen through the eyes of the main protagonist – the desperately shy fifteen-year-old Kambili, this </span>
  <span id="freeText9817897091669536663" style="display:none">Whilst not quite in the same league as ‘Half of a Yellow Sun’ (‘Purple Hibiscus’ is neither as accomplished nor as ambitious in scope) – ‘Purple Hibiscus’ is nevertheless a very strong and affecting novel.<br /><br />Set again in Nigeria and although told against a backdrop of civil unrest and corruption, this is very much focussed on the family and on the characters immediate domestic situation. Told by, and seen through the eyes of the main protagonist – the desperately shy fifteen-year-old Kambili, this is to some extent a coming of age story about growing up in a privileged family, experiencing violence at home along with all the complicated and complex emotions associated with that. There is perhaps another contrast here with ‘Half of a Yellow Sun’ – where the violence (whilst affecting all) was experienced and committed largely on and driven by the bigger political stage.<br /><br />As with ‘Half of a Yellow Sun’, there are good, strongly written and believable characters here who you want to know more about. Again there is a feeling of authenticity and believability to this novel.<br /><br />Other important and broader themes central to the story of ‘Purple Hibiscus’ and covered well, focus on religious oppression and its associated divisions, the clash between the old and the new, the traditional and the modern, sin and retribution, the divisions within family and in society, privilege and disadvantage – maybe the main theme is just that – division and the struggle for reconciliation? <br /><br />This is a thought provoking and engaging novel covering universal themes – recommended.<br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9817897091669536663" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_219094416" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/219094416" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/219094416?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/126381.Purple_Hibiscus">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=location-nigeria">location-nigeria</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2017">read2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=location-africa">location-africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=postal-bookswap">postal-bookswap</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=around-the-world">around-the-world</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer219094416" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3779498478355451280">I was pleased to open this book as one of the picks during a year + with the "Postal Book Swap F" group. This is our second year send books around and the picks are entirely secret until everyone has seen everything. <br/><br/>I had previously read and enjoyed <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15796700.Americanah' title='Americanah by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie' rel='nofollow noopener'>Americanah</a> and always thought I might go back and read Adichie's previous works. I also have <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18749.Half_of_a_Yellow_Sun' title='Half of a Yellow Sun by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie' rel='nofollow noopener'>Half of a Yellow Sun</a> on my shelf, unread.<br/><br/>I loved this story, and it resonated deeply because of my own experiences with my own father. And I think she does </span>
  <span id="freeText3779498478355451280" style="display:none">I was pleased to open this book as one of the picks during a year + with the "Postal Book Swap F" group. This is our second year send books around and the picks are entirely secret until everyone has seen everything. <br /><br />I had previously read and enjoyed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15796700.Americanah" title="Americanah by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie" rel="nofollow noopener">Americanah</a> and always thought I might go back and read Adichie's previous works. I also have <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18749.Half_of_a_Yellow_Sun" title="Half of a Yellow Sun by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie" rel="nofollow noopener">Half of a Yellow Sun</a> on my shelf, unread.<br /><br />I loved this story, and it resonated deeply because of my own experiences with my own father. And I think she does a good job of showing that no matter how much privilege you have, if you aren't safe at home, you do not have a good life. I really felt for Kambili and Jaja. <br /><br />(Thanks Connie!)</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2311805482?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krista Regester" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krista Regester" href="/user/show/53724042-krista-regester">Krista Regester</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/126381.Purple_Hibiscus">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2311805482" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2945999256496471703"><i>Purple Hibiscus</i> is a brilliant read, and Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie writes with understated passion. I love the story and how the family interacts with each other is so interesting. <br/><br/>Why I have to give it a 4 star: Lisette Lecat narrated the audio. Although she is a south African native... she is white... and she has a British accent. I find this pretty inappropriate and distracting since this entire book is based on a young girl of color and her family. Because of this, I felt I was unable to conn</span>
  <span id="freeText2945999256496471703" style="display:none"><i>Purple Hibiscus</i> is a brilliant read, and Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie writes with understated passion. I love the story and how the family interacts with each other is so interesting. <br /><br />Why I have to give it a 4 star: Lisette Lecat narrated the audio. Although she is a south African native... she is white... and she has a British accent. I find this pretty inappropriate and distracting since this entire book is based on a young girl of color and her family. Because of this, I felt I was unable to connect to the story as well. Not to mention all of the intense swallowing and gulping that was happening. Come on, you can't edit that out?? It sounded like she was having to take long breaks throughout where she would just gulp down a huge swig of water. I was pretty surprised and disappointed by this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2945999256496471703" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1450957077" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1450957077" />
    <a title="Emma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28602001-emma"><img alt="Emma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438967931p2/28602001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1450957077?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emma" href="/user/show/28602001-emma">Emma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007268382" href="/book/show/1780191.Purple_Hibiscus">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1450957077" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4512705141314510246">Adichie has an incredible talent for making the reader lose themselves in the story she has created. I could feel the gritty winds of the harmattan, and the bumpy, potholed roads between Enugu and Nsukka; see the blooming purple hibiscus and the dancing Mmuo spirits. I loved Adichie's inclusion of Igbo words, contextualised or explained so that I was never uncertain of their meaning. I actually had more trouble with the vocabulary of Catholicism, not being religious myself, and had to look up ma</span>
  <span id="freeText4512705141314510246" style="display:none">Adichie has an incredible talent for making the reader lose themselves in the story she has created. I could feel the gritty winds of the harmattan, and the bumpy, potholed roads between Enugu and Nsukka; see the blooming purple hibiscus and the dancing Mmuo spirits. I loved Adichie's inclusion of Igbo words, contextualised or explained so that I was never uncertain of their meaning. I actually had more trouble with the vocabulary of Catholicism, not being religious myself, and had to look up many of the terms. Yet it was these small additional details that enhanced the sense of time and place in the novel. It made it all the more real to read the brief, yet violent moments of physical abuse. The family home was full of the tension and silence that is born of fear. What a comparison it made to the home of Aunty Ifeoma, where anything could be said and laughter filled the space. <br /><br />In dealing with themes such a familial assault, political uncertainty, wealth, colonialism, religion, and morality, Adichie has created a book that is both specific and universal. Her skilful writing and personal experience place the setting firmly within the book's Nigerian setting, but the examples and lessons she draws from human character have a far wider relevance.<br /><br />While reading other reviews, I noticed that many seem to suggest that this is the worst of her books. If so, I say, brilliant...I already have both <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/576650.Half_of_a_Yellow_Sun" title="Half of a Yellow Sun by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie" rel="nofollow noopener">Half of a Yellow Sun</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20939933.Americanah" title="Americanah by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie" rel="nofollow noopener">Americanah</a> to read next.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4512705141314510246" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2575727678" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2575727678" />
    <a title="Sonja Arlow" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6449756-sonja-arlow"><img alt="Sonja Arlow" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576564309p2/6449756.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2575727678?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sonja Arlow" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sonja Arlow" href="/user/show/6449756-sonja-arlow">Sonja Arlow</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6449756-sonja-arlow?shelf=recommended">recommended</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6449756-sonja-arlow?shelf=2018-read">2018-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6449756-sonja-arlow?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6449756-sonja-arlow?shelf=africa-themed">africa-themed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2575727678" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4799192090366362391">3.5 stars<br/><br/>There is something unique yet familiar about this coming of age story.<br/><br/>Kambili and her brother Jaja grows up in luxury with a highly respected father during a time period where Nigeria is under military reign. But behind closed doors this father rules with an iron fist and almost fanatical religious zeal.<br/><br/>The atmosphere of living with an abusive parent was captured so well that it made the reading difficult at times. The author also shows the destructive nature of holding on to a belief t</span>
  <span id="freeText4799192090366362391" style="display:none">3.5 stars<br /><br />There is something unique yet familiar about this coming of age story.<br /><br />Kambili and her brother Jaja grows up in luxury with a highly respected father during a time period where Nigeria is under military reign. But behind closed doors this father rules with an iron fist and almost fanatical religious zeal.<br /><br />The atmosphere of living with an abusive parent was captured so well that it made the reading difficult at times. The author also shows the destructive nature of holding on to a belief to the detriment of your humanity.<br /><br />What makes this unique is the setting. With vivid descriptions of life in Nigeria and specifically the food, it made me want to try Nigerian cuisine….. well maybe not the Aku (termites)<br /><br />The story is told from the viewpoint if Kambili and she came across as both naïve and world weary.<br /><br />The ending felt a little abrupt for me but overall I think it’s a worthy addition to the YA genre that will expose western readers to the charms of Nigeria but also show that we all have the same hopes and fears.<br /><br />As a side note the author’s TED talks are amazing and highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4799192090366362391" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_1863269229'><a id="like_count_review_1863269229" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1863269229?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">29 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F14569052-purple-hibiscus">Like</a></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1171380632" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1171380632" />
    <a title="Candi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3639005-candi"><img alt="Candi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585187464p2/3639005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1171380632?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Candi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Candi" href="/user/show/3639005-candi">Candi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/126381.Purple_Hibiscus">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=africa">africa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1171380632" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12989935629490423885"><i>“I laughed because Nsukka’s untarred roads coat cars with dust in the harmattan and with sticky mud in the rainy season. Because the tarred roads spring potholes like surprise presents and the air smells of hills and history and the sunlight scatters the sand and turns it into gold dust. Because Nsukka could free something deep inside your belly that would rise up to your throat and come out as a freedom song. As laughter.”</i><br/><br/>This debut novel by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie is full of expressive prose </span>
  <span id="freeText12989935629490423885" style="display:none"><i>“I laughed because Nsukka’s untarred roads coat cars with dust in the harmattan and with sticky mud in the rainy season. Because the tarred roads spring potholes like surprise presents and the air smells of hills and history and the sunlight scatters the sand and turns it into gold dust. Because Nsukka could free something deep inside your belly that would rise up to your throat and come out as a freedom song. As laughter.”</i><br /><br />This debut novel by Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie is full of expressive prose just as brilliant as this one uttered by Kambili upon her return visit to the one place that gave her a voice and a chance to understand what life could really be outside the judgmental and abusive authority of her religiously fanatic father, Eugene. Fifteen-year old Kambili and her older brother Jaja lead a prosperous but sheltered life with this authoritarian father and their submissive mother. Domestic abuse and violence are sadly a recurrent theme in their household, and many incidents left me feeling quite angered and sorry for the children and their mother. Kambili narrates, <i>“Fear. I was familiar with fear, yet each time I felt it, it was never the same as the other times, as though it came in different flavors and colors.”</i> Despite the fear, however, Kambili respects her father and works very hard to earn his approval and love. <i>“I knew that when the tea burned my tongue, it burned Papa’s love into me.”</i> Jaja, on the other hand, while a victim of this abuse as well, is introduced as having a bit more of a self-assertive personality, beginning with a refusal to take communion at church one Sunday. This rebellious nature grows quite rapidly throughout the book. <i>“Jaja’s defiance seemed to me now like Aunty Ifeoma’s experimental purple hibiscus: rare, fragrant with the undertones of freedom, a different kind of freedom from the one the crowds waving green leaves chanted at Government Square after the coup. A freedom to be, to do.”</i><br /><br />There is a great contrast in this novel between the Igbo culture of the children’s grandfather, Papa Nnukwu, and the Catholic fervor of Eugene, who is Papa Nnukwu’s son. Eugene goes so far as to call his own father “heathen” and “pagan” due to his unwillingness to convert to Catholicism and will not allow Papa Nnukwu within the walls of his own home. His children are not allowed to see their grandfather save for the very rare several minute visits that are permitted to them. Kambili is confused by this and struggles with the conflict between her conviction in her father’s views and with her own private thoughts about her grandfather. <i>“I had examined him that day, too, looking away when his eyes met mine, for signs of difference, of Godlessness. I didn’t see any, but I was sure they were there somewhere. They had to be.”</i> When Kambili and Jaja leave their parents to stay for several days in Nsukka with Aunty Ifeoma, Eugene’s forward-thinking sister and university professor, they finally get a taste for what it is like to be out from under the strain of their father’s watchful eye. Aunty Ifeoma is a strong presence and her three children live a completely different life from that of their cousins Kambili and Jaja. At the dinner table, Kambili thinks <i>“I had felt as if I were not there, that I was just observing a table where you could say anything at any time to anyone, where the air was free for you to breathe as you wished.”</i> Her cousins are allowed to speak their mind without the fear of unreasonable punishment. Catholic followers as well, this family does not exhibit any of the fanaticism we see in Eugene. While staying in Nsukka, the children are also introduced to Father Amadi who shows them a gentle and caring side to Catholicism. He takes Kambili under his wing and gives her a reason to finally use her voice. She learns about love and happiness as she develops a very strong emotional attachment to Father Amadi. <i>“I had smiled, run, laughed. My chest was filled with something like bath foam. Light. The lightness was so sweet I tasted it on my tongue, the sweetness of an overripe bright cashew fruit.”</i> This relationship was the most poignant part of the novel, in my opinion. <br /><br />Now for some minor faults I noted in this book. I had a bit of difficulty settling into this initially. It took some time for me to become engaged with the characters. While the writing was superb, I felt that the characters were a bit too slowly developed. Although this was told from Kambili’s perspective, I had a hard time attaching myself to her during the first part of the novel. While I certainly felt very sympathetic towards Kambili and her mother and brother, I didn’t feel a real connection but had a sense of distance from them. This eventually changed further along in the book, at least with Kambili, but was a negative point for me at first. I also felt the plot was a bit loose at times. Sometimes events felt drawn out while others were rushed. Perhaps these little flaws are a result of this being Adichie’s first novel. The Nigerian setting is absorbing and the language is just so lovely; I am very hopeful that any further work written by Adichie can only have improved. She did have an interesting twist to the story which I enjoyed as well, but won’t divulge any more here. These positive points all point me in the direction of reading her next piece of writing. 3.5 stars go to <b>Purple Hibiscus</b>.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12989935629490423885" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_910187800" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/910187800" />
    <a title="Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas"><img alt="Thomas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567953607p2/2018505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/910187800?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/126381.Purple_Hibiscus">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=own-physical">own-physical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer910187800" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12712981238772833073">A great coming-of-age story about fifteen-year-old Kambili, an obedient girl who watches as Nigeria falls under a military coup. At the same time her own family struggles to keep their personal cracks sealed. Kambili's father, a man who values religion above all else, abuses Kambili and her brother, ignores their ailing pagan grandfather, and helps hundreds of poor people all at once. When her father sends Kambili and her brother away to stay with their educated aunt and her free-spirited childr</span>
  <span id="freeText12712981238772833073" style="display:none">A great coming-of-age story about fifteen-year-old Kambili, an obedient girl who watches as Nigeria falls under a military coup. At the same time her own family struggles to keep their personal cracks sealed. Kambili's father, a man who values religion above all else, abuses Kambili and her brother, ignores their ailing pagan grandfather, and helps hundreds of poor people all at once. When her father sends Kambili and her brother away to stay with their educated aunt and her free-spirited children, Kambili learns another way of life - one that makes her question her past devotion.<br /><br />A valuable book that delves into Nigerian culture, religion, and politics while focusing on Kambili's growth as a character. Adichie's writing possesses a natural rhythm that made me fall into her story, and the exploration of Kambili's changing ties - both to her family and her spiritual beliefs - felt honest and moving. My favorite part of <i>Purple Hibiscus</i> was Kambili's connection to her father: Adichie's characterization of their relationship shows that no human only contains black or white. Evil and good can share space in the same body and mind; the actions of Kambili's father exemplifies that duality.<br /><br />Overall, a solid work of cultural, literary fiction. Kambili's voice felt a bit too dulled for me at certain points, but I would still recommend <i>Purple Hibiscus</i> to fans of <i>The Color Purple</i> or <i>A Thousand Splendid Suns</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12712981238772833073" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1562131148" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1562131148" />
    <a title="Sarah (Presto agitato)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1257768-sarah-presto-agitato"><img alt="Sarah (Presto agitato)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294588421p2/1257768.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1562131148?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah (Presto agitato)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah (Presto agitato)" href="/user/show/1257768-sarah-presto-agitato">Sarah (Presto agitato)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19086439-purple-hibiscus">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1257768-sarah-presto-agitato?shelf=africa">africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1257768-sarah-presto-agitato?shelf=nigeria">nigeria</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1562131148" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4730607544943918768">Chimamanda Adichie is one of those rare writers who has a gift for <i>seeing</i> as much as for writing. Her prose is evocative yet precise, and the story is carefully structured and well-paced. The most striking aspect of this novel, though, is the nuance of the characterizations. The main characters are all multi-layered, with aspects of their personalities revealed a little at a time, quietly, resulting in a picture that is rich and real. Even minor characters who make only brief appearances, like t</span>
  <span id="freeText4730607544943918768" style="display:none">Chimamanda Adichie is one of those rare writers who has a gift for <i>seeing</i> as much as for writing. Her prose is evocative yet precise, and the story is carefully structured and well-paced. The most striking aspect of this novel, though, is the nuance of the characterizations. The main characters are all multi-layered, with aspects of their personalities revealed a little at a time, quietly, resulting in a picture that is rich and real. Even minor characters who make only brief appearances, like the woman who braids hair at the market sitting next to her basket of snails, leave an impression. Adichie has a way of making you look twice at small details with a subtle yet revelatory emphasis.<br /><br />This isn't a story of absolutes. Here, as in life, good people do bad things, bad people do good things, religious sincerity can result in both great kindness and terrible cruelty, and a country can be both a beloved home and a place of repression. The choice of responses on both the family level and on a larger scale is the same--resist? submit? escape?--but there are no easy answers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4730607544943918768" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/540018614" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3820197-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1383385041p2/3820197.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/540018614?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/3820197-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/126381.Purple_Hibiscus">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jill by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/110812-kinga">Kinga</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3820197-jill?shelf=kinga-forced-me">kinga-forced-me</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer540018614" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6958064984162067914"><br/><b>3.5 stars</b><br/><br/>Kambili is fifteen, living at home with her brother, Jaja, her mother and her father, a wealthy businessman. Their home life though affluent and seemingly stable is an unhappy one with Kambili, Jaja and their mother walking on eggshells, living with the physically and emotionally abusive father, a religious, fanatical tyrant. Nigeria, politically unstable at this time, succumbs to a military coup.<br/><br/>This is author, Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie's debut. The writing is flowing, easy to follow, t</span>
  <span id="freeText6958064984162067914" style="display:none"><br /><b>3.5 stars</b><br /><br />Kambili is fifteen, living at home with her brother, Jaja, her mother and her father, a wealthy businessman. Their home life though affluent and seemingly stable is an unhappy one with Kambili, Jaja and their mother walking on eggshells, living with the physically and emotionally abusive father, a religious, fanatical tyrant. Nigeria, politically unstable at this time, succumbs to a military coup.<br /><br />This is author, Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie's debut. The writing is flowing, easy to follow, though not simplistic or superficial and sounded much like the educated, intelligent teenage narrator, Kambili should. The drama plays out within the family with the political strife set in the background.<br /><br />Perhaps because I've had too many encounters in my life with fanatical, religious hypocrites this story didn't sit too well with me. While I acknowledge the story was interesting even compelling, I could put it down (often) to clean or iron. Though this is told through the eyes of fifteen year-old Kambili, the central character seemed to me to be her father whose presence loomed menacingly over almost every page even when he wasn't featured in the scene. <br /><br />I think <i>Purple Hibiscus</i> is well worth reading, the novel itself well-crafted, though for me it was overshadowed by my dislike of the overbearing, dogmatic, rigid zealot who equates religion with God. The ending put a gleeful smirk on my face. <br /><br />* Thanks Kinga for the recommendation<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6958064984162067914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer532121180" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5746797571919577228">This was a great book to read. <br/><br/>Chimamanda Ngozi Adiche combined Nigerian politics, religion, cuisine, traditional believes and industry in such a way that neither of the elements overshadowed the story of the fifteen-year old Kambili and her family. Although her father was religiously rigid, physically, mentally and emotionally abusive to the family, especially Kambili's mom, Adiche still showed his softer side of him caring for so many hundreds of people either openly or anonymously. Her wealth</span>
  <span id="freeText5746797571919577228" style="display:none">This was a great book to read. <br /><br />Chimamanda Ngozi Adiche combined Nigerian politics, religion, cuisine, traditional believes and industry in such a way that neither of the elements overshadowed the story of the fifteen-year old Kambili and her family. Although her father was religiously rigid, physically, mentally and emotionally abusive to the family, especially Kambili's mom, Adiche still showed his softer side of him caring for so many hundreds of people either openly or anonymously. Her wealthy industrialist father uses religion to suppress and abuse his family, like so many millions of other people in the world who do the same thing.<br /><br />Kambili experiences love and abuse from the same person, she experience intellectual stimulation as well traditional believes and learn how to cope with it all through the different family relationships. <br /><br />She fears her father's uncompromising overpowering persona, especially his rigid staunch devotion to a Catholic God, yet falls in love with a celibate priest as her first ever love, which makes it a gentle and kind first experience for a girl. The customs, lifestyles and Nigerian landscape is almost unobtrusively presented to the reader through feminine eyes. Although the book tends to be initially a little slow moving, it still has enough drama and intrigue to capture the attention until the very last word is written.<br /><br />It really is a great great great read!<br /> <br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_2457754520" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2457754520" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2457754520?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inderjit Sanghera" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera">Inderjit Sanghera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2457754520" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5533478052500086499">The delicate and decorous flowering of the purple hibiscus represents the journey of the narrator, Kambili, from shy, insecure teenager, wilting under her domineering father, lonely and lachrymose, for whom words are inadequate representations for her feelings of isolation and insecurity, to a woman who is enlivened by love, which acts like the gentle rain-fall on a verdant spring morning in causing her dormant inner emotional life to bloom and spring forth, awakened by the carefree Father Amadi</span>
  <span id="freeText5533478052500086499" style="display:none">The delicate and decorous flowering of the purple hibiscus represents the journey of the narrator, Kambili, from shy, insecure teenager, wilting under her domineering father, lonely and lachrymose, for whom words are inadequate representations for her feelings of isolation and insecurity, to a woman who is enlivened by love, which acts like the gentle rain-fall on a verdant spring morning in causing her dormant inner emotional life to bloom and spring forth, awakened by the carefree Father Amadi, whose brown eyes act as a mirror for Kambili to recognise her dormant beauty, like the floresence of a purple hibiscus flower in the village of her aunt.<br /><br /> Kambili's father Eugene is a complex character; one the one-hand he is a humble philanthropist, a man who cares deeply about the society around him, about helping others, however from the start there a streak of self-hatred in his selflessness; a sense that his desire to help is in fact a colonial desire to civilise, to rescue his countrymen from their barbarity, his outward confidence a mask for the deep-seated insecurities which plague him, causing him to tyrannise his family, to treat his pagan father with a callousness which can only be a product of religious fervour, a man obsessed with dogma and, paradoxically, for all the outward showings of humility, his own ego. And that is the tragic centre of the story; that a man who is essentially good is so trapped by toxic ideas on masculinity and colonialism that his actions frequently belie his natural goodness.<br /><br />Though wider than this, perhaps, 'Purple Hibiscus' is about the masks we wear and the superficial trappings of happiness brought about by prosperity; although rich and successful, Eugene's household is one of repression and violence, whereas that of his sister is freedom and peace, of love and understanding in place of parochialism. And it is love, whether it be Ifeoma's love for her father or the love which blooms between Kambili and Amada which bring her happiness and give her life meaning.<br /><br />Whilst not Adichie's masterpiece, 'Purple Hibiscus' is a skilfully women story of life on post-colonial Nigeria, of the politics and dissidence, of the discombobulation brought about by the loss of their native culture, language and traditions in the face of an alien Western culture, of a young girl who is brought back to life by the joyous, boyish smile of a priest whose idea of Christianity is one of joy and understanding in contrast with her dogmatic and domineering father. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5533478052500086499" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3099140357" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3099140357" />
    <a title="Ash" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57274851-ash"><img alt="Ash" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603895397p2/57274851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3099140357?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ash" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ash" href="/user/show/57274851-ash">Ash</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57274851-ash?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57274851-ash?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57274851-ash?shelf=read-2020">read-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57274851-ash?shelf=top-2020">top-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3099140357" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4146911873782815761">This book surprised me with how good it was. I’d read plenty of praise for Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie, so I knew her writing would be good, but I wasn’t expecting it to blow me away like it did. This book earned an easy five stars for the way it effortlessly evoked strong emotion and changed my worldview. There is so much contained in just these 300-ish pages.<br/><br/><i>Purple Hibiscus</i> is a frighteningly realistic depiction of abuse. Protagonist Kambili is the teenage daughter of a wealthy Nigerian businessm</span>
  <span id="freeText4146911873782815761" style="display:none">This book surprised me with how good it was. I’d read plenty of praise for Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie, so I knew her writing would be good, but I wasn’t expecting it to blow me away like it did. This book earned an easy five stars for the way it effortlessly evoked strong emotion and changed my worldview. There is so much contained in just these 300-ish pages.<br /><br /><i>Purple Hibiscus</i> is a frighteningly realistic depiction of abuse. Protagonist Kambili is the teenage daughter of a wealthy Nigerian businessman who uses his devout Catholic beliefs as justification for the way he treats his wife and two children. The tension in this family’s household, the atmosphere of fear and denial, and Kambili’s desperation to please her father leapt off the page. I came away from this book with a better understanding of the far-reaching, destructive effects abuse has on individuals and families.<br /><br />Outside of Kambili’s immediate family, <i>Purple Hibiscus</i> features a small but well-developed cast of side characters, including Kambili’s aunt and cousins, who demonstrate to Kambili and her brother Jaja what life is like in a happy, healthy home environment. There’s also Father Amadi, a young priest and a character I’m very ambivalent about. He was very likable, but <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I was uncomfortable with the budding romantic relationship between him and Kambili, given not just their age gap but also their obvious gap in maturity and life experience <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />What makes this book shine is Adichie’s incredibly unique and effective writing style. It’s straightforward and sparse, which in some ways is even more impressive than lavish, wordy prose; it takes a rare writer to communicate so much emotion and meaning with so few words. It reads like a real teenage girl’s voice, but without sounding immature or otherwise putting off an adult reader.<br /><br />Although the theme of abuse had the biggest impact on me, <i>Purple Hibiscus</i> is about so much more than that. It effectively explores the ways religion can be used for good or twisted for evil, the way Europeans used religion as a tool of colonization in Africa, and the state of human rights and the rule of law in Nigeria. Adichie’s writing contains countless eye-opening observations that reminded me of my own limited worldview.<br /><br /><i>Purple Hibiscus</i> is a worthwhile and enjoyable read, and I can think of few people I wouldn’t recommend it to. I’ll add my voice to the many before me who have praised Adichie for her accomplishment with this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4146911873782815761" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('APQbQdNUTY58piD/VQBistrX5WlATqXrLikSPBAQ4CaCPN/TJc9EM4J8FYSmM1Q0+WEN+FGCMUEdSn3x1vS+NQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9ngLRKILV+jBu1iS92b2NQKagxXtDQEXpUWa6TC6SEd0sM/WVJBeVT9hbekEVcCzISxrhPzBlb2WJvUk9l4WVA==')})
  }

Extraordinary for its exquisite language, its implacable sorrow, its soaring beauty, and for its m<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13533377159563870514\" style=\"display:none\">A novel of breathtaking sweep and emotional power that traces three hundred years in Ghana and along the way also becomes a truly great American novel. Extraordinary for its exquisite language, its implacable sorrow, its soaring beauty, and for its monumental portrait of the forces that shape families and nations, Homegoing heralds the arrival of a major new voice in contemporary fiction.\n\nTwo half-sisters, Effia and Esi, are born into different villages in eighteenth-century Ghana. Effia is married off to an Englishman and lives in comfort in the palatial rooms of Cape Coast Castle. Unbeknownst to Effia, her sister, Esi, is imprisoned beneath her in the castle\'s dungeons, sold with thousands of others into the Gold Coast\'s booming slave trade, and shipped off to America, where her children and grandchildren will be raised in slavery. One thread of Homegoing follows Effia\'s descendants through centuries of warfare in Ghana, as the Fante and Asante nations wrestle with the slave trade and British colonization. The other thread follows Esi and her children into America. From the plantations of the South to the Civil War and the Great Migration, from the coal mines of Pratt City, Alabama, to the jazz clubs and dope houses of twentieth-century Harlem, right up through the present day, Homegoing makes history visceral, and captures, with singular and stunning immediacy, how the memory of captivity came to be inscribed in the soul of a nation. \n\nGeneration after generation, Yaa Gyasi\'s magisterial first novel sets the fate of the individual against the obliterating movements of time, delivering unforgettable characters whose lives were shaped by historical forces beyond their control. Before their homes were destroyed by paramilitary policemen, before the school closed, before the fathers left for dangerous jobs abroad.\n\nBut Darling has a chance to escape: she has an aunt in America. She travels to this new land in search of America\'s famous abundance only to find that her options as an immigrant are perilously few.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie">About Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1474446019p3/11291.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16115355690587457924">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie grew up in Nigeria.<br/><br/>Her work has been translated into over thirty languages and has appeared in various publications, including <i>The New Yorker</i>, <i>Granta</i>, <i>The O. Henry Prize Stories</i>, the <i>Financial Times</i>, and <i>Zoetrope</i>. She is the author of the novels <i>Purple Hibiscus</i>, which won the Commonwealth Writers’ Prize and the Hurston/Wright Legacy Award; <i>Half of a Yellow Sun</i>, which won t</span>
  <span id="freeText16115355690587457924" style="display:none">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie grew up in Nigeria.<br /><br />Her work has been translated into over thirty languages and has appeared in various publications, including <i>The New Yorker</i>, <i>Granta</i>, <i>The O. Henry Prize Stories</i>, the <i>Financial Times</i>, and <i>Zoetrope</i>. She is the author of the novels <i>Purple Hibiscus</i>, which won the Commonwealth Writers’ Prize and the Hurston/Wright Legacy Award; <i>Half of a Yellow Sun</i>, which won the Orange Prize and was a National Book Critics Circle Award Finalist and a <i>New York Times</i> Notable Book; and <i>Americanah</i>, which won the National Book Critics Circle Award and was named one of <i>The New York Times</i> Top Ten Best Books of 2013. Ms. Adichie is also the author of the story collection <i>The Thing Around Your Neck</i>.<br /><br />Ms. Adichie has been invited to speak around the world. Her 2009 TED Talk, <i>The Danger of A Single Story</i>, is now one of the most-viewed TED Talks of all time. Her 2012 talk <i>We Should All Be Feminists</i> has a started a worldwide conversation about feminism, and was published as a book in 2014.<br /><br />Her most recent book, <i>Dear Ijeawele, or a Feminist Manifesto in Fifteen Suggestions</i>, was published in March 2017.<br /><br />A recipient of a MacArthur Foundation Fellowship, Ms. Adichie divides her time between the United States and Nigeria.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16115355690587457924" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
